<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="informing-the-dying-patient">Informing the Dying Patient<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#informing-the-dying-patient" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Most patients realise even without being told that they are suffering from an incurable disease. This may be through non-verbal communication of the patients' family and health personnel. Patients feel isolated, lonely and rejected when the truth is withheld. Informed patients usually react negatively at first. Nonetheless, there are several advantages when the truth is revealed. These include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Maintaining all relationships with the patients on the basis of honesty rather than sustaining the false premise that recovery will occur.</li>
<li>Providing the patient with the opportunity to complete unfinished business.</li>
<li>Permitting the use of still unidentifiable inner resources that have been demonstrated to prolong life 'the will to live'.</li>
<li>Promoting more meaningful communication between the patient, family and health care personnel.</li>
<li>Resolving grief earlier and more effectively. Grief is resolved well when individuals state they want say and do things with the dying patient that they would later regret not having done.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="patterns-of-emotional-reactions">Patterns of Emotional Reactions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#patterns-of-emotional-reactions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Studies have shown that there is a common pattern of responding to the knowledge of an impending death. Dr. Elisabeth Kubler Ross describes stages that a dying person experiences as follows:</p>
<p>The Stages of Dying according to Dr. Elisabeth Kubler Ross</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Stage</th><th>Typical emotional response</th><th>Typical comment</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>First stage</td><td>Denial</td><td>â€œNo, not me.â€ The patient may think there has been a mistake.</td></tr><tr><td>Second stage</td><td>Anger</td><td>â€œWhy me?â€ the patientâ€™s hostility may be directed toward family members, friends or health workers.</td></tr><tr><td>Third stage</td><td>Bargaining</td><td>â€œYes me, but...â€ The bargain may be a promise to go, if the patient is a religious person, in exchange for more time or a person may say he will do anything in exchange for such experiences as seeing a child graduate from school enjoying his next birthday.</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Bishops Computers
Bishops Computers</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Fourth stage</th><th>Depression</th><th>â€œYes me,â€ the patient feels sadness and often cries as though they is mourning their own death.</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Fifth stage</td><td>Acceptance</td><td>â€œI am readyâ€. A positive feeling and a readiness characterise the comment for death. This stage is usually peaceful and tranquil.</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Note that not all persons go through these stages in the order described in the Stages of Dying table. A person may skip one of those stages, or fall back a stage. Stages may overlap. The length of any stage may range from a few hours to months. Knowing about these stages will assist you to understand and help patients and the families cope with dying. Here are some suggestions Kubler Ross and others have offered on how to help patients through the stages of dying:</p>
<ul>
<li>Accept whatever manner in which the patient responds.</li>
<li>Provide a non-judgemental atmosphere in which the patient can express their feelings freely.</li>
<li>Be ready to listen, especially at night when patients tend to awaken and want to talk.</li>
<li>Work to understand the patient's feelings.</li>
<li>Provide the patient with a broad opening for communication such as "Do you want to talk about it?" This allows the patient to choose the topic that they wish to discuss.</li>
</ul>
<p>A terminally ill patient may require continuous hospitalisation, home care or hospice care. You should be able to anticipate the services needed by the family and assist in obtaining them. You should be able to provide support and continuity of care at home</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="home-care">Home Care<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#home-care" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>When coordinating home care, make sure that:</p>
<ul>
<li>The family and the patient prefer home care to other options</li>
<li>The family and the patient are aware of the patient's diagnosis and prognosis</li>
<li>A support system is available for professional consultation and help with care as necessary</li>
<li>Any special equipment needed for the patient is availed</li>
<li>A person is ready and able to assume primary responsibility for the patient's care</li>
</ul>
<p>Generally, terminally ill patients cared for at home, compared to those in hospital, enjoy greater emotional comfort and dignity and their families adjust better to their dying and death.</p>
<p>When their family members in a familiar environment surround the terminally ill patient, they feel more secure. The family members are able to communicate and demonstrate their love and affection without any intimidation. Having family members care for the patient lessens guilty feelings. Children can participate more extensively in the last days and can be helped to understand death with less fear.</p>
<p>The process of dying is a gradual one. Family members can start working through some grieving phases. When the care of the dying patient is too complex or very demanding for family members, hospice care is recommended when available.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="hospice-care">Hospice Care<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hospice-care" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Hospice care as defined by Dr. Cicely Saunders of England, is a way station where terminally ill persons can live out their final days with dignity and meaningfulness in a caring environment. Hospice care emphasises helping the patient live until they die with their family with them and helping the family return to normal living after the patients' death. Hospice cares also emphasises preserving a bond between the patient and their family and helping them to be prepared so that death can be more readily accepted as a normal part of life.</p>
<p>If you are not familiar with the term hospice care there is a facility of this nature at the Kenyatta National Hospital, Nairobi and Moi Teaching and Referral Hospital, Eldoret and others are gradually coming up.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="responding-to-emotional-needs">Responding to Emotional Needs<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#responding-to-emotional-needs" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A dying patient requires emotional support. The patient's helpless feelings cause them to depend upon others to provide them with a sense of being safe, secure loved and worthwhile. You can help the patient and their family by sustaining realistic hope, understanding common fears, facilitating communication and helping the family accept reality.</p>
<p>Bishops Computers
Bishops Computers</p>
<p>Bishops Computers
Page 63 of 425
Bishops Computers</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="sustaining-unrealistic-hope">Sustaining Unrealistic Hope<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sustaining-unrealistic-hope" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Realistic hope involves a wish for something that is more than likely possible. The patient, the family and health care personnel will manifest some degree of hope which may not be realistic nor in agreement with one another. Nonetheless, unrealistic hope should be supported. You should provide statement like 'we will continue to do everything we can and hope you are right about your expectations'. This comment neither shatters hope nor supports unrealistic hope.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="understanding-common-fears">Understanding Common Fears<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#understanding-common-fears" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Fears are as varied as attitudes toward death. Most people fear death because it represents a force over which there is no control. Some may choose to fight it, while others look forward to death as a relief from earthly suffering and sorrow. Yet others feel so depressed and desperate that they have suicidal tendencies. You are required to relieve the patient's fear concerning death so as to facilitate moving to stage of acceptance in which the patient can die in peace and dignity.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-common-fears-of-dying">The Common Fears of Dying<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-common-fears-of-dying" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Abandonment</th><th>Many dying patients feel isolated and alone. You should encourage the family to be present and involved. It relieves the feeling of separation from others.</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Extreme pain</td><td>Discomfort that cannot be relieved is both physically and emotionally exhausting. Provide measures for controlling pain.</td></tr><tr><td>Loss of control</td><td>This may be related to inability to control bodily functions such as faecal or urinary elimination, diminished intellectual capacity or the inability to maintain a previously held role within the family unit.</td></tr><tr><td>Dependence</td><td>Most adults resent having to depend on others for functions that once were performed independently.</td></tr><tr><td>Body alterations</td><td>Some terminal illness is treated by the surgical removal of body structures. Drug therapy may cause hair loss or other changes in the patient's overall appearance. The patient may feel that they will repulse others.</td></tr><tr><td>Loss of dignity</td><td>Dying patients fear being treated as an object rather than a person.</td></tr><tr><td>Financial ruin</td><td>Medical expenses are bound to accumulate during a lengthy illness. The potential for leaving one's family penniless is a common fear.</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="facilitating-communication">Facilitating Communication<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#facilitating-communication" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>A patient may verbalise statements that mask their true feelings or may talk in symbolic language. The comments may be made to determine if others can tolerate talking about a certain subject. They may also be used as a substitute for revealing thoughts that are too frightening to discuss. Open communication between you, the patient and the family can help individuals to cope and deal with the reality of issues concerning death.</p>
<p>You may need to interpret what a patient may be trying to say abstractly. Non-verbal communication often conveys messages more clearly than verbal communication. What you should do is to: 'listen to what is being said'. Important clues about feelings or ideas that they are too frightened to express openly may be understood in the following ways:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Paraphrase or restate what may be the message.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Provide time for the patient to perceive confirm or deny the interpretations.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Pace the conversation, but do not press the patient to communicate.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Too slow a response may be equated with disinterest.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Firing questions may be associated with insensitivity.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="accepting-reality">Accepting Reality<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#accepting-reality" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>There comes a time when it becomes apparent that death will no longer be delayed. The patient and their family will find comfort in knowing this. The patient's discomfort is eased if they can express how meaningful and appreciated the dying person has been.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="meeting-spiritual-needs">Meeting Spiritual Needs<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#meeting-spiritual-needs" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Many terminally ill patients find great comfort in the support they receive from their religious faith. Help the patient in obtaining the services of a religious leader.</p>
<p>A minister, rabbi, sheikh, or priest is one of the team assisting the patient to face terminal illness. You should continue to provide kind words and a gentle touch.</p>
<p>Bishops Computers
Page 64 of 425
Bishops Computers</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="attending-to-physical-needs">Attending to Physical Needs<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#attending-to-physical-needs" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>You have a responsibility of helping the patient meet their physical needs, which include providing nourishment, maintaining elimination, administering hygiene, controlling pain, protecting from harm, modifying environment and involving family members in the patients' care.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="providing-nourishment">Providing Nourishment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#providing-nourishment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The terminally ill patient has little interest in nourishment.</p>
<p>The physical effort of eating and drinking are very taxing to them. Nausea or vomiting may interfere with adequate food consumption. Consequently, poor nutrition leads to exhaustion, infection and other complications such as developing pressure sores. The patient may take fluids or tube feeding to maintain nutrition and fluid intake. When death is near, the normal activities of the gastrointestinal tract decrease. If you offer large quantities of food, the patient may get distension of the abdomen, which adds to the discomfort.</p>
<p>Offer frequent sips of water if the swallowing reflex is present.</p>
<p>As swallowing becomes difficult, aspiration may occur when fluids are given. The patient may be able to suck on gauze soaked in water or ice chips wrapped in gauze without difficulty because sucking is one of the last reflexes to disappear as death approaches.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="maintaining-elimination">Maintaining Elimination<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#maintaining-elimination" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Some patients may be incontinent of urine and faeces. Others may be having retention of urine and constipation.</p>
<p>Provide cleansing enemas, but remember that if a patient is taking little nourishment there may be little amounts of faecal material in the intestine. Cauterise at regular intervals.</p>
<p>An indwelling catheter may be necessary for some patients. Take care to maintain skin care if the patient is incontinent of urine and faeces to prevent odours and decubitus ulcers. Waterproof bedpans are easier to change than bed linen.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="ensuring-proper-hygiene">Ensuring Proper Hygiene<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ensuring-proper-hygiene" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Keep the dying patient clean, well groomed and free of unpleasant odour to maintain the dignity of the patient. This can be done by:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Maintaining oral hygiene</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Maintaining eye care</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Maintaining skin care by sponging them and keeping them dry to promote relaxation and quiet sleep as well as cleanliness, please note that a complete bath may be tiring and cause extreme discomfort</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Maintaining wound care if applicable</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Keeping the bed linen and bed clothing dry</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Keeping the patient's hair clean and groomed</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="positioning-the-patient">Positioning the patient<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#positioning-the-patient" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Maintain proper positioning of the patient. Ensure there are frequent changes in position. Put the patient in semi-sitting position if dyspnoeic. Place the patient on their side if they have noisy breathing to keep the tongue from obstructing the airway.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="controlling-the-pain">Controlling the Pain<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#controlling-the-pain" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Keep the patient free from pain but do not dull their consciousness or ability to communicate. Control pain when it is minimal and before it becomes intense to the point where it requires larger doses of analgesics.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="protecting-from-harm">Protecting from Harm<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#protecting-from-harm" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>In order to protect the patient from harm:</p>
<ul>
<li>Put aside the rails on the bed if the patient is restless</li>
<li>Allow the patient's relatives to remain with them so that they do not injure themselves</li>
<li>You should maintain a close watch on the patient</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="modifying-the-environment">Modifying the Environment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#modifying-the-environment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Modifying the environment involves:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Placing the patient in a room close to the nursing station for the convenience of giving nursing care and so as to observe them at frequent intervals</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Making the patient's room meaningful to them, this enables the patient to feel more comfortable and secure</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Provide pictures, books and other significant objects</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Using normal lighting, terminally ill patients often complain of loneliness and fears which may be as a result of poor vision exaggerated by darkening the room</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Ensuring that the room is well ventilated</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Speaking to the patient in a normal tone, as whispers may mean to them that there are some secrets being hidden</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Taking care of the topics of conversation, the sense of hearing is the last to leave the body and even when the patient appears to be unconscious, the patient may hear what is being said in their presence</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>It is comforting to the patient for others to say things that they may like to hear even when they cannot respond. It is kind and thoughtful to speak to them. You should explain to the patient what care will be given so that they do not misunderstand the actions or become fearful.</p>
<p>Additionally, family members should be involved, where possible, in the patient's care for the following reasons:</p>
<ul>
<li>Family members often appreciate helping with the patient's care</li>
<li>They feel helpless and welcome the opportunity to assist the patient. Their cooperation helps to maintain a family bond</li>
<li>Family members often find that helping with care improves their ability to cope with the situation</li>
<li>It also helps to begin and promote the grieving process</li>
</ul>
<p>It is important, however, to inform the family that they can call for nursing assistance at any time. You should be sensitive to the amount of care and involvement the family can undertake.</p>
<p>The overall responsibility and accountability of the patient's care is yours.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="how-would-you-recognise-the-signs-of-approaching-death">How would you recognise the signs of approaching death?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#how-would-you-recognise-the-signs-of-approaching-death" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Most persons die gradually over a period of hours or days. Human cells cease to live when there is lack of sufficient oxygen. The process of dying has signs that indicate clearly that death is imminent. You shall now look at some of these signs.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="paranormal-experiences">Paranormal Experiences<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#paranormal-experiences" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The patient may relate visions of having seen, talked with or been in the presence of family members or friends that have died. They may have been visited by a religious figure such as an angel.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="motion-and-sensation-are-lost">Motion and Sensation are Lost<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#motion-and-sensation-are-lost" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This begins with the extremities of the feet and legs. The normal activities of the gastrointestinal tract begin to decrease and</p>
<p>reflexes gradually disappear. The jaw and facial muscles relax and the patient's expression, which may have appeared anxious, becomes peaceful. The eyes may remain partly open.</p>
<p>Although the patient's temperature is usually elevated, they feel cold and clammy.</p>
<p>This begins with their extremities and the tip of the nose, reflecting the beginning of circulatory collapse.</p>
<p>Respiration may be noisy and the death rattle may be heard. This may be due to an accumulation of mucus in the respiratory tract when the patient is no longer able to raise and expectorate sputum. Cheyne Stokes respiration commonly occurs. Circulation fails and the blood pressure falls. The patient's skin becomes cyanotic, grey and/or pale. The pulse becomes irregular, weak and rapid.</p>
<p>As the level of consciousness changes, the brain may no longer perceive pain. The patient's mental condition usually deteriorates. They may become confused and disoriented. Complete unconsciousness and coma may occur, although some patients remain conscious until death. You should remember when giving care that the amount of mental alertness varies among patients.</p>
<p>You may have realised in your practice that some family members request to be summoned when the patient's condition deteriorates.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="how-would-you-handle-such-a-situation-if-it-presents-itself-here-are-a-few-suggestions">How would you handle such a situation if it presents itself? Here are a few suggestions:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#how-would-you-handle-such-a-situation-if-it-presents-itself-here-are-a-few-suggestions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Summon the family, giving your name, title and indicate where the call is being made from</li>
<li>Determine the identity of the person who has answered the telephone</li>
<li>Explain that you are calling because the patient's condition has worsened</li>
<li>Speak in a calm and controlled voice to reassure the relative that you are in command of the serious nature of the patient's condition</li>
<li>Use short sentences to provide small bits of information. You may have realised that it is difficult for a relative under stress to follow lengthy technical explanations with understanding</li>
<li>Pause to allow the receiver of the call time to comprehend. Inform the family member the care that is being provided at the moment</li>
</ul>
<p>Bishops Computers
Bishops Computers</p>
<ul>
<li>Urge the relative to come at once to the hospital</li>
<li>Document the time and the individual to whom you have communicated the information</li>
</ul>
<p>If a death has occurred, the doctor should be responsible for informing the patient's nearest relative</p>
<p>The information should be delayed until the family member arrives in order to avoid precipitating any desperate acts such as suicide or contributing to a traffic accident.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="how-would-you-handle-the-relative">How would you handle the relative?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#how-would-you-handle-the-relative" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Meet the relative you talked to over the telephone to allow continuity in communication. Do not leave the family alone and try to provide privacy, if possible, because they may weep and sob uncontrollably. Do not be surprised if emotions are not easily displayed, because if the family has had a period of time to anticipate the patient's death, much of the emotion already is spent.</p>
<p>Expect a severe emotional reaction if a dying person has been a victim of a sudden and unexpected accident. This is because the family members have not had time to prepare for the loss. You need to provide emotional support, such as, allowing them to express their grief and listening to them as they vent their feelings</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="confirming-death">Confirming Death<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#confirming-death" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>You may determine that a patient is dead when there is no evidence of pulse, respiration or blood pressure. Pupils will be dilated and fail to respond to light. A doctor, by law, is the one to pronounce the patient dead.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="obtaining-permission-for-an-autopsy">Obtaining Permission for an Autopsy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#obtaining-permission-for-an-autopsy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>An autopsy (post-mortem examination) is an examination of the organs and tissues of a human body. It is usually performed if the death was of a suspicious nature, involved a crime, and/or occurred without any medical consultation prior to death.</p>
<p>An autopsy cannot be performed without the written consent of the next of kin. It is the doctor's responsibility to obtain permission for an autopsy.</p>
<p>The procedure involves delicate communication, tact and compassion. Many relatives will be more comfortable if they are told that the reason for performing autopsy is to establish proof of the exact cause of death.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="issuing-a-death-certificate">Issuing a Death Certificate<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#issuing-a-death-certificate" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The laws of Kenya require that a death certificate be issued for each person who has died. The laws specify the information that is needed. Notification of death is usually sent to the Registrar of Births and Deaths. This information is used to compile statistics, which are important in identifying trends, needs and problems in the fields of health and medicine.</p>
<p>Look for a death notification form and check the information itemised.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="nursing-responsibilities-following-the-patient-s-death">Nursing Responsibilities Following the Patient's Death<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nursing-responsibilities-following-the-patient-s-death" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>You should always remember that you are still involved with both the patient and the family even after death occurs. The relatives may wish to view the body if the death occurred in their absence. You should ask them if they would wish to be left alone or if they do not mind you accompanying them. If the former, do not be too distant.</p>
<p>It is human for you to become involved and attached to patients and their families. Many families are touched that the nurse has also shared their loss. You should not fight to control expressing how you feel. At times you may not have words of comfort, but just listening and allow them time to reminisce or express their emotions.</p>
<p>You should encourage the survivors of a sudden death to view the body, seeing and touching the body confirms the reality of death. You should clean and cover mutilated areas.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="accounting-for-valuables">Accounting for Valuables<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#accounting-for-valuables" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Every hospital has policies about the care of valuables when patients are admitted. Get a copy from the administration of your hospital. There are some valuables, which a patient chooses to keep with them, for example, a ring, a wristwatch or money. These need careful handling. Occasionally, the patient's family take the valuables home when death becomes imminent. Make sure you note it down in the patient's notes and they sign for them.</p>
<p>If valuables are still with the patient at the time of death, they should be identified, accounted for, and sent to the appropriate department for safe keeping until the family claims them. A wedding ring should not be removed, but should be secured with adhesive so that it does not slip off and get lost. You should note that the ring remained on the body.</p>
<p>Bishops Computers
Bishops Computers</p>
<p>Loss of valuables is serious and may result in a lawsuit. You should, therefore, take every precaution to prevent loss or misplacement of valuables.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="section-3-clinical-diagnosis">SECTION 3: CLINICAL DIAGNOSIS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#section-3-clinical-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="introduction">Introduction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In section two, you studied some basic nursing procedures.</p>
<p>In this section you will cover how to diagnose and treat minor illnesses. You will also briefly look at a new concept. In addition to the Integrated Management of Adolescents and Adult Illness (IMAI), you may have already heard of the Integrated Management of Childhood Illness (IMCI). This will be covered in unit four of module one on paediatric nursing.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="introduction">Introduction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In section two, you studied some basic nursing procedures.</p>
<p>In this section you will cover how to diagnose and treat minor illnesses. You will also briefly look at a new concept. In addition to the Integrated Management of Adolescents and Adult Illness (IMAI), you may have already heard of the Integrated Management of Childhood Illness (IMCI). This will be covered in unit four of module one on paediatric nursing</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="objectives">Objectives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#objectives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>By the end of this section you will be able to:</p>
<ul>
<li>Obtain a comprehensive history from a patient</li>
<li>Perform a physical examination on a patient</li>
<li>Interpret the findings from history taking and physical examination</li>
<li>Formulate a clinical diagnosis</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-health-history">The Health History<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-health-history" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The scope of nursing practice has expanded to include some activities that were once strictly for the doctor or other members of the health care team. The nurse now employs skills in developing the patient's database by performing physical examination as a step in the nursing process. In certain situations, you will be expected to diagnose and treat minor illness. While conducting a nursing history you will look at the patient's psychological and cultural patterns, the interpersonal and physical environments, as well as the patient's</p>
<p>life style and activities of daily living. You will be responsible for developing a database through: a detailed history of the patient's present illness; past medical history; family history and review of body systems. It will be beneficial to include: individual and family relationships; life style patterns; health practices and coping strategies.</p>
<p>What you should take note of during history taking is:</p>
<ul>
<li>Ethical considerations in data collection</li>
<li>Communication skills and interviewing techniques</li>
<li>Content of the health history</li>
</ul>
<p>Now move on to look at these components one by one.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="ethical-considerations-in-data-collection">Ethical Considerations in Data Collection<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ethical-considerations-in-data-collection" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In unit one of this module you looked at legal aspects in nursing and the nursing process, and you covered the patients' rights.</p>
<p>Ethical considerations in data collection emphasise that the patient has the right to know why information is being collected and how it will be used. You are therefore expected to identify yourself and your role, provide a detailed explanation of what a health history is, how the information will be elicited and how it will be used.</p>
<p>You are also expected to inform the patient on all aspects of the data collection process and of their freedom to participate in/abstain from the process. Try and provide a private setting for the interview. This will promote an atmosphere of trust between the patient and yourself. It will also encourage open and honest communication.</p>
<p>Remember to only selectively record data that is pertinent to the patient's health status. Highly sensitive information should not be entered on the record but can be discussed with persons with authority at the health facility. Always remember to secure written records from the public or those health professionals not directly involved in the care of the patient.</p>
<p>Confidentiality and maintaining a high standard of nursing care and professional conduct is paramount.</p>
<p>In obtaining a personal history from the patient you will be required to utilise basic interviewing skills. The details of this will be covered in unit five of module one.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="communication-skills-and-interviewing-techniques">Communication Skills and Interviewing Techniques<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#communication-skills-and-interviewing-techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It is important to make patients feel at ease by making them comfortable and giving them respect. Reassure the patients that all the information shared is confidential. Only health professionals directly involved in the care will</p>
<p>Bishops Computers
Bishops Computers</p>
<p>get access to it. Ensure a private setting for the interview.</p>
<p>Allow for the self expression of the patient so as to obtain facts that will influence spontaneous information flow. Promote the patient's full expression through non verbal communication. For example, you could encourage the patient to elaborate or continue by a nod of the head or by repeating the last few words if the patient is hesitant.</p>
<p>A puzzled look will encourage the patient to clarify inconsistencies in the history. Pose open ended questions such as "Tell me about it", "How did it feel?", "How can we help you?" and so on.</p>
<p>Be careful! At times the patient may provide you with what they thinks you want to hear. You should vary the style of questioning by including a few direct questions such as "Does the pain come before the meal, during the meal or after the meal?" You should use direct questioning when the patient has developed a sense of trust and confidence in you.</p>
<p>You should use a health history form to guide the interview but adjust the sequence of questions to coincide with the flow of the conservation. Listen to the patient as they answer the questions. Take brief notes during the interview and maintain eye contact with the patient.</p>
<p>You should demonstrate an understanding of the nature and intensity of the patient's problem.</p>
<p>The manner in which you respond non verbally to the patient and your ability to listen should convey a genuine willingness to understand the meaning of the patient's concern. The patient should be reassured so that the interview is no longer viewed as an enquiry but feels therapeutic.</p>
<p>Do not interrupt the patient's silent or tearful episodes. Resist verbal reassurances during these moments.</p>
<p>Encourage the patient to elaborate on their feelings by making open ended statements such as "You look sad" or "You seem to be frightened". Convey an understanding of, and respect for, the patient's beliefs and attitudes. Adopt a non judgemental attitude when dealing with issues related to sexuality, drug and alcohol use or cultural patterns.</p>
<p>Take into account the patient's cultural background. Remember, cultural attitudes about family relationships and the role of a woman are accepted at face value, just as attitudes toward pain, illness and hospitalisation are accepted.</p>
<p>"These beliefs and attitudes are derived from personal experiences which vary according to the person's cultural background." (Brunner &amp; Suddarth, 1988).</p>
<p>You should also be aware of your own feelings and attitudes.</p>
<p>You should always try not to convey irritation, boredom or disbelief towards a patient's behaviour even though it may be offensive or morally unacceptable to you.</p>
<p>Interpret non verbal communication appropriately, for example, gestures that convey defensiveness, hostility, confidence or impatience. Also, learn to respond to body language.</p>
<p>You should make an effort to communicate in a manner that is consistent with the individual's level of understanding.</p>
<p>You should take into consideration the patient's educational background. Use as few technical terms as possible. Take into consideration the patient's cultural attitudes towards health</p>
<p>Terminate an interview in an appropriate manner that summarises the information obtained and ensures that the patient has understood the major points discussed.</p>
<p>Always inquire whether the patient has any questions. Search for areas of misunderstandings by briefly summarising the patient's responses. This allows the patient to correct areas of misinformation. They may also add facts they had forgotten to mention earlier.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="content-of-the-health-history">Content of the Health History<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#content-of-the-health-history" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Having looked at the basic guidelines for interviewing, move on to look at the content of the interview.</p>
<p>The content will include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Biographical data</li>
<li>Informant data</li>
<li>Chief complaint</li>
<li>Past medical history</li>
<li>History of present illness</li>
<li>Review of systems</li>
<li>Family history</li>
<li>Patient profile</li>
<li>Physical examination</li>
<li>Radiological and laboratory information</li>
<li>Problem formulation</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="biographical-data">Biographical Data<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#biographical-data" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This information helps to put the history of the patient into context.</p>
<p>The information includes:</p>
<ul>
<li>Name</li>
<li>Address</li>
</ul>
<p>Bishops Computers
Bishops Computers</p>
<ul>
<li>Age</li>
<li>Sex</li>
<li>Marital Status</li>
<li>Occupation</li>
<li>Ethnicity</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="informant-data">Informant Data<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#informant-data" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="list-at-least-three-situations-when-the-patient-may-not-be-the-informant">List at least three situations when the patient may not be the informant<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#list-at-least-three-situations-when-the-patient-may-not-be-the-informant" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The patient may not be the informant if they fall into one or more of these categories:</p>
<ul>
<li>A child</li>
<li>An elderly person</li>
<li>An unconscious patient</li>
<li>A patient suffering from a severe psychiatric disturbance</li>
</ul>
<p>You should assess the reliability of the informant and the usefulness of the information provided. For example, a hysterical or depressed patient may not provide reliable information whereas patients who abuse drugs and alcohol may use denial as part of their operating mechanism.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="chief-complaint">Chief Complaint<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chief-complaint" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The chief complaint is the issue that brings the patient to seek medical help.</p>
<p>Ask questions such as "What brings you to the clinic today?"</p>
<p>There could be variations in getting a chief complaint. The patient may have come for just a check up or may have more than one main problem.</p>
<p>List the problems in order of priority. Then expose them and separate them as separate entities.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="past-medical-history">Past Medical History<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#past-medical-history" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A comprehensive summary of the patient's previous medical history is very important. This is because the past could have triggered the present, or the present is a continuation or a complication in the past. Ask the patient about: immunisation status; any known allergies to drugs or other substances; last physical examination; chest X-ray; eye examination; hearing examination; dental check up or papanicolaou smear (if female). For previous illnesses, record any negative or positive responses. Elicit a history of the following areas:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Childhood illnesses: rubella, polio, whooping cough, mumps, chicken pox, rheumatic fever, sore throat</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Adult illnesses</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Psychiatric illness</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Injuries: burns, fractures, head injuries</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Hospitalisation</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Operations</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Current medications, including prescriptions, over-the-counter and/or home remedies</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>If the hospitalisation or major medical intervention is related to the present illness, indicate "see history of present illness" on the data sheet</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="history-of-present-illness">History of Present Illness<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#history-of-present-illness" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>You will need to reflect on your knowledge of the patho-physiology and natural history of disease when exploring the facts related to a present illness. This will enable you to arrive at a diagnosis.</p>
<p>The physical examination will reveal manifestations unfolded in the history laboratory and radiological information may be helpful. However their selection will be based on the history obtained.</p>
<p>You should compile the history of present illness as a complete story indicating:</p>
<ul>
<li>The date and manner of the onset of the problem, that is, whether sudden or gradual</li>
<li>The setting in which the problem developed, that is, whether at home, at work or after an argument; manifestations of the problem</li>
<li>Course of illness or problem</li>
</ul>
<p>The course of illness includes self-treatment, medical interventions, progress and effects of treatment and the patient's perception of the cause or meaning of the problem.</p>
<p>You should delineate in detail specific symptoms such as pain, headache, fever and change in bowel habits. For example, if pain is identified, make a critical analysis about its location and radiation, quality, severity and duration. You should pursue the persistence or intermittence of the symptom, factors that aggravate or alleviate it and any associated manifestations that the patient may be aware of. The associated manifestations are symptoms that occur simultaneously with the chief complaint.</p>
<p>The absence or presence of associated symptoms may point to the origin or extent of the patient's problem, as well as on the diagnosis. These symptoms are referred to as significant positive or negative findings, derived from a review of systems directly related to the chief complaint.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="example">Example<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#example" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>If a patient complains of a vague symptom such as fatigue or weight loss, you should review all body systems and include the</p>
<p>Bishops Computers
Bishops Computers</p>
<p>information in the history of the present illness. If the patient's chief complaint is chest pain, both gastrointestinal and cardiovascular systems may be included in the history of present illness.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="in-either-situation-record-both-positive-and-negative-findings">In either situation, record both positive and negative findings.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#in-either-situation-record-both-positive-and-negative-findings" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="family-history">Family History<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#family-history" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Take the history of the health status or the age and cause of death of parents, siblings, spouse, children and grandparents, cousins and so on, to identify diseases that may be hereditary, communicable or environmental.</p>
<p>You should specifically inquire about such conditions like cancer, hypertension, kidney disease, arthritis, allergies, asthma, alcoholism and obesity.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="review-of-systems">Review of Systems<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#review-of-systems" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The review of systems includes a complete inventory of major body organ systems in</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>General</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Loss or gain of weight</td></tr><tr><td>Appetite change</td></tr><tr><td>Night sweats</td></tr><tr><td>Weakness</td></tr><tr><td>Fatigue</td></tr><tr><td>Fever</td></tr><tr><td>Skin</td></tr><tr><td>Rash</td></tr><tr><td>Colour change</td></tr><tr><td>Dryness</td></tr><tr><td>Nail change</td></tr><tr><td>Hair change</td></tr><tr><td>Growth or masses</td></tr><tr><td>Pruritis</td></tr><tr><td>Head</td></tr><tr><td>Headache</td></tr><tr><td>Trauma</td></tr><tr><td>Dizziness</td></tr><tr><td>Eyes</td></tr><tr><td>Vision (near and far)</td></tr><tr><td>Glasses or contact</td></tr><tr><td>Diplopia</td></tr><tr><td>Pain</td></tr><tr><td>Infection</td></tr><tr><td>Itching</td></tr><tr><td>Blurring</td></tr><tr><td>Ears</td></tr><tr><td>Hearing</td></tr><tr><td>Pain</td></tr><tr><td>Infection</td></tr><tr><td>Excessive cerumen</td></tr><tr><td>Hygiene practices</td></tr><tr><td>Tinnitus</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>terms of presence or absence of symptoms, past or present. It serves as a check and balance so as to prevent you from overlooking any relevant data. You should record negative and positive responses. Taking into account the history of present illness, analyse the symptom the patient has responded positively to. You should get an overview of general health as well as symptoms related to each body system.</p>
<p>You could use a checklist as part of the health history to review the systems. The advantage of a checklist is that it can be easily audited and is less subject to error. In the checklist circle any positive responses and underline the negative responses to indicate the presence or absence of the symptom.</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="patient-profile">Patient Profile<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#patient-profile" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The profile is useful for the analysis of the patient's problem, their capacity to deal with the problem and the health care team's capacity to provide assistance. The information to be obtained is personal and subjective. It includes the expression of feelings, values and personal experiences. A general patient profile consists of:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="past-development">Past Development<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#past-development" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Past Development includes:</p>
<ul>
<li>Place of birth</li>
<li>Place lived</li>
<li>Significant childhood/adolescent experiences</li>
</ul>
<p>Past Development includes:</p>
<ul>
<li>Place of birth</li>
<li>Place lived</li>
<li>Significant childhood/adolescent experiences</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="education-and-occupation">Education and occupation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#education-and-occupation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Education and occupation includes:</p>
<ul>
<li>Jobs held in the past</li>
<li>Current position/job</li>
<li>Length of time at position</li>
<li>Education preparation</li>
<li>Work satisfaction and career goals</li>
<li>Financial resources</li>
<li>Insurance coverage</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="physical-environment">Physical Environment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#physical-environment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Physical environment can be either spiritual or interpersonal.</p>
<p>Other factors to be considered are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Ethnic background (language spoken, customs and values held, folk practices used to maintain health or to cure illness)</li>
<li>Family relationships (family structure, roles, communication patterns, support systems)</li>
<li>Friendships (quality of relationships)</li>
</ul>
<p>Physical environment can be either spiritual or interpersonal.</p>
<p>Other factors to be considered are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Ethnic background (language spoken, customs and values held, folk practices used to maintain health or to cure illness)</li>
<li>Family relationships (family structure, roles, communication patterns, support systems)</li>
<li>Friendships (quality of relationships)</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="lifestyle-patterns-and-habits">Lifestyle (patterns and habits)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#lifestyle-patterns-and-habits" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Lifestyle patterns are of importance and include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Sleep (time individual retires, hours per night, comfort measures, awakens rested?)</li>
<li>Exercise (type, frequency, time spent)</li>
<li>Nutrition (24 hour diet recall, restrictions, idiosyncrasies)</li>
<li>Recreation (type of activity, time spent)</li>
<li>Caffeine (coffee, tea, cola, chocolate, kind and amount)</li>
<li>Smoking (cigarettes, pipe, cigar, marijuana) kind, amount per day, number of years, desire to quit</li>
<li>Alcohol - kind, amount, pattern over past year</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="self-concept">Self-concept<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#self-concept" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Questions should include:</p>
<ul>
<li>View of self in present</li>
<li>View of self in future</li>
<li>Body image (level of satisfaction, concerns)</li>
<li>Sexuality: perception of self as a man or woman, quality of sexual relations</li>
<li>Concerns related to sexuality or sexual functioning</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="stress-response">Stress response<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#stress-response" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Monitor stress response. This involves:</p>
<ul>
<li>Major concerns or problems at present</li>
<li>Past experiences with similar problems</li>
<li>Past coping patterns and outcomes</li>
<li>Present copying strategies and anticipated outcomes</li>
<li>Individual's expectations of family/friends and health care team in problem resolution</li>
</ul>
<p>You have looked at the outline of the patient's profile.</p>
<p>You will now look at the significance of each subtopic of the profile</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="past-development">Past Development<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#past-development" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The reason for you to question the patient's place of birth and places they lived in the past will help them to focus on the earlier years of their life.</p>
<p>Personal experiences during childhood and adolescence may highlight personal achievement, a failure, a developmental crisis, any incidence of physical or emotional abuse or a valued</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="education-and-occupation">Education and Occupation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#education-and-occupation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>You may have already experienced that it is very difficult to solicit information related to economical status or educational preparation from a patient.</p>
<p>You are advised to approach such issues indirectly by asking questions such as "Tell me</p>
<p>about your job" or "Do you have any financial concerns at this time?"</p>
<p>It is not necessary to know the exact numerical value of the person's salary. Get information as to whether their income is sufficient to meet their expenses and support the lifestyle they are used to. You could also touch on their insurance coverage and plans for health care payment.</p>
