Bishops Computers</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="drug-effects">Drug Effects<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#drug-effects" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="desired-therapeutic-effects">Desired Therapeutic Effects<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#desired-therapeutic-effects" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The desired therapeutic effects should indicate the mechanism of action of the drug.</p>
<p>For example, an analgesic is for pain relief, accomplished by central nervous system depression inhibition of inflammation, neutralisation of acid in the stomach, vasodilation in angina or muscle relaxation. You must therefore understand how medication affects body function in order to make sound judgements about which medication to use.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="side-effects">Side Effects<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#side-effects" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Side effects of a drug are physiological effects exerted by the chemical that are not related to the desired therapeutic effect. You must, therefore, be familiar with serious side effects and commonly occurring side effects.</p>
<p><strong>Remember: All drugs have side effects. The number and range of side effects may indicate the relative toxicity of a given medication.</strong></p>
<p>Adverse reactions include any undesirable effect apparent in the recipient. They may be opposite to the desired effect, allergic or extraneous. Unusual effects seen in some patients due to individual differences are known as idiosyncratic, which refers to the tendency within the individual to react unfavourably to certain substances.</p>
<p>When you know that a patient has an idiosyncrasy, or where a group of drugs is known to precipitate such reactions, extra care must be exercised. For example, iodine and iodides will precipitate coryza. Aspirin may cause urticaria and asthma. Barbiturates may cause a measles like rash.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="toxic-effects">Toxic Effects<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#toxic-effects" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Toxic effects are those that involve an excessive drug effect. They tend to be exaggerations of therapeutic or side effects. For example, toxicity from a central nervous system depressant used for sedation may induce a coma. If the depressant is an opiate, which acts as a miotic, the pupils are reduced to pinpoint size. The margin of safety between therapeutic and toxic doses varies greatly.</p>
<p>What you should know is that any substance that is physiologically potent enough to produce therapeutic effects is potentially harmful to the body at certain circumstances. Toxic symptoms could appear in clients receiving appropriate dosages. This is because drug response, metabolism and excretion vary from person to person.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="contraindications">Contraindications<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#contraindications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Contraindications are conditions or symptoms that alert you to the potential dangers of the drug. You must always decline to administer any drug that you believe will cause harm to the client. In such cases, let the physician administer the drug personally. In such a situation, there are legal implications and this is the reason for the decision not to administer the drug.</p>
<p>Take a look at these Drug Standards one by one.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="dosage-range">Dosage Range<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#dosage-range" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The usual dosage must be included for each method of administration. Dosages require adjustment according to certain factors for example:</p>
<ul>
<li>Body mass</li>
<li>Nutritional status</li>
<li>Pathologic condition</li>
<li>Patients' ability to metabolise and excrete the drug</li>
</ul>
<p>When you notice any unusual dosages, you should verify and clarify with the doctor. You should refuse to carry out an order that, in your judgment, will result in harm to the patient. Errors in dosage are not uncommon especially in the case of verbal orders. It is your responsibility to alert the doctor to the possibility of error.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="elimination">Elimination<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#elimination" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>You must be aware of the physiological mechanisms by which a drug is inactivated and eliminated from the body. The efficiency of elimination affects the efficacy and potential for toxicity of a given medication. Many drugs are deactivated by microsomal enzymes in the liver and excreted by the kidneys. You must always adhere to procedures that promote accurate identification of drugs before administering them to patients.</p>
<p>Drugs are derived from plants, animals or mineral resources. Others are produced synthetically in the laboratory. These drugs may be in various forms. You have been administering various preparations of drugs.</p>
<p>How many preparations of drugs can you recall?</p>
<p>The Different Preparations of Drugs</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Preparation</th><th>Description</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Mixture</td><td>A liquid preparation consisting of more than one drug dissolved in water or other fluids.</td></tr><tr><td>Draught</td><td>A mixture dispensed as a single dose usually 50ml.</td></tr><tr><td>Elixir</td><td>A pleasantly flavoured liquid preparation, frequently containing alcohol as an adjunct to other flavouring.</td></tr><tr><td>Emulsion</td><td>A suspension of two immiscible liquids, for example, oil and water, one dispersed in the other.</td></tr><tr><td>Syrup</td><td>A concentrated solution of sugar in water.</td></tr><tr><td>Linctus</td><td>A cough sedative in liquid preparation.</td></tr><tr><td>Tincture</td><td>A drug dissolved in spirit for either internal or external use</td></tr><tr><td>Solution</td><td>A drug dissolved in a liquid.</td></tr><tr><td>Lotion</td><td>An aqueous mixture for external use.</td></tr><tr><td>Liniment</td><td>A lotion with the property of counter irritation.</td></tr><tr><td>Capsule</td><td>Contains powder or liquid enclosed in gelatine.</td></tr><tr><td>Tablet</td><td>Compressed powder.</td></tr><tr><td>Pill</td><td>A solid spherical body containing medicinal agents in a base.</td></tr><tr><td>Ointment</td><td>A semi-solid medication preparation in a greasy base.</td></tr><tr><td>Paste</td><td>An ointment with a high proportion of an insoluble powder in it, which makes it stiff and difficult to spread.</td></tr><tr><td>Cream</td><td>A semi-solid emulsion.</td></tr><tr><td>Lamellae</td><td>Small gelatinous discs used in the eye or under the tongue.</td></tr><tr><td>Suppositories</td><td>Conical shaped bodies made of gelatin or cocoa butter, containing drugs for introduction into the rectum.</td></tr><tr><td>Pessaries</td><td>Similar to suppositories but larger, for introduction into the vagina</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The Route of Administration</p>
<p>The route of administration is dependent on the time at which the effect is required. It is also dependent upon the method most suitable to the drug prescribed. Where possible, oral administration is preferred. Drugs can be administered orally, sublingually, rectally or by injection.</p>
<ul>
<li>Orally, where drugs are administered orally in liquid, powder or tablet form. Sublingually, where tablets or lamellae may be allowed to dissolve under the tongue.</li>
<li>Rectally, which involves the use of suppositories or enemata.</li>
<li>Inhalation, where drugs are inhaled as vapour or sprays.</li>
<li>Locally or topically, where drugs are used locally on the skin or in the eye, ear or vagina.</li>
<li>Injection, which can be done in several ways including intradermally, subcutaneously, intramuscularly, intravenously and intrathecally.</li>
</ul>
<p>You must bear in mind that when prescribing a drug, the dosage may vary with certain factors.</p>
<p>The Factors to Bear in Mind When Prescribing a Drug</p>
<p>Age</p>
<p>The extremes of age that is, children and adults over 60 years require smaller doses than the normal adult dose. You must, therefore, calculate their drug dosage as per kilo of body weight.</p>
<p>The drug dosages are normally indicated at the label of the drug container or the literature accompanying the drug. In common practice, there is an assumed dosage for children over thirteen years and adults. You should note that calculation of drug dosages will also depend on the age. For children and adults over 65 years it is advisable to calculate the dosages per kilo of body weight. Follow the physician's or manufacturers instructions when you are calculating the drugs dosages.</p>
<p>Route</p>
<p>The route by which the drug may be given varies and the drug dosage may also vary according to the route of administration.</p>
<p>Assimilation</p>
<p>The assimilation of drugs given orally is fairly slow, thus the oral dose is large, whereas any</p>
<p>Bishops Computers
Bishops Computers</p>
<p>drug injected into the blood stream acts immediately and dosage is proportionately smaller.</p>
<p>Therefore, you must take the greatest care to check the target cells. Organise concentration so that you administer drugs by the correct route.</p>
<p>As you prepare to administer drugs, you have to be conversant with the knowledge of weights and measures.</p>
<p><strong>Note the following examples of weights and measures carefully!</strong></p>
<p>1000 micrograms (mcg) = 1 milligram (mg)
1000 milligrams (mg) = 1 gramme (g)
1000 grammes (g) = 1 kilogram (kg)
1000 millilitres (ml) = 1 litre (L)</p>
<p>Also remember, that while you are in the community you may be forced to convert the measurements into domestic equivalents.</p>
<p>A teaspoonful is nearly 5ml
A tablespoonful is nearly 10ml
A teacupful is nearly 150ml
A tumblerful is about 250ml</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-administration-of-medication">The Administration of Medication<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-administration-of-medication" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Administration involves all activities related to safe drug use which include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Assessing the risk to a patient of a new drug order</li>
<li>Delivering the drug dose to the proper body tissues</li>
<li>Assessing the patient's response to drug therapy</li>
<li>Treatment of adverse reactions to drugs</li>
<li>Consultation with the doctor about adjusting the prescribed regimen</li>
<li>Educating the patient about the proper use of drug substances</li>
</ul>
<p>All these are your responsibilities as a nurse.</p>
<p>You are also expected to administer oral and topical drugs, as well as parental drugs and those injected directly into the vascular system.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-five-rights">The Five Rights<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-five-rights" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Accuracy in drug administration depends on five factors: the right drug; the right dose; the right patient; the right route and the right time. You shall now look at each of these factors in greater detail.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-right-drug">The Right Drug<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-right-drug" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>To ensure that you have used the right drug,</p>
<p>you must check and double check the package label and the cardex and medication card or sheet. You must prepare the medications you give yourself and do not administer drugs prepared by someone else.</p>
<p><strong>The person who administers the medication is the one held responsible.</strong></p>
<p>You should recheck the order, the label and the medication card if a client questions the medication. A mentally alert client will notice a change in medication or mention problems that have arisen from the medication.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="do-not-ignore-statements-or-questions-regarding-medication-from-a-client">Do not ignore statements or questions regarding medication from a client!<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#do-not-ignore-statements-or-questions-regarding-medication-from-a-client" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Please ensure that you take the following precautions when administering medicine:</p>
<ul>
<li>All doses are best prepared from the original container</li>
<li>Medicines should not be prepared in the dark. Good illumination is necessary for positive identification</li>
<li>You should caution clients about the use of non labelled pillboxes</li>
<li>Do not to mix supplies of several tablets or capsule in a single container</li>
<li>Make sure you check medication labels when removed from the shelf, before pouring or measuring and when returning to the shelf</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-right-dose">The Right Dose<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-right-dose" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>To obtain the right dose, you must carefully measure the medicine. Use the proper technique for pouring solid drugs such as capsules and tablets in order to minimise handling of the drugs. You should pour the medication into the container's cap, and then transfer the required number of units in the dose from the cap to the medication cup. If you require half of a tablet, a scored tablet may be cut into two pieces with a knife edge or folded in clean paper and broken with the fingers.</p>
<p>Scored tablets may be split by exerting digital pressure on each side of the slot. Fold tissue or a paper towel around the tablet to avoid contact between the hands and the medication. This procedure is easiest with larger tablets. Small tablets, which provide too little leverage for the fingers, should be cut with a knife. Do not attempt to split non scored tablets or to divide the dose of a single capsule. When you split tablets, give the two halves in successive doses, so that any deviation from the prescribed dose due to uneven breakage is levelled out as quickly as</p>
<p>Bishops Computers
Bishops Computers</p>
<p>possible. Do not break all the tablets available and mix the halves.</p>
<p>Liquids should be measured in a container with a scale that provides a mark for the required dose, for example, plastic medicine glasses or spoons.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-right-client">The Right Client<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-right-client" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>You should make sure that the right client receives the right drug. You should only give drugs to the person for whom they are prescribed or recommended for.</p>
<p>If the patient is wearing an identification bracelet, check the client's name on their identification bracelet with the name on the medication card in your hand. Alternatively, if the patient is conscious and sane, simply call out the patient's name. This check is essential to avoid errors.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-right-route">The Right Route<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-right-route" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The right route must be used for drug delivery. Most medicines are taken orally or by topical application. Ensure that the client understands how the drug is to be taken. Sublingual or chewable tablets should not be swallowed whole. Crush oral drugs, if swallowing is difficult or if they are to be taken in liquid form. Demonstrate to the client procedures for application of topical drugs.</p>
<p><strong>Giving medication by the wrong route can cause death. The person who administers the drug is held responsible.</strong></p>
<p>You should always check the doctor's orders and the cardex or treatment sheet to verify the medication route. Alert the doctor if the route is not in accord with that which is recommended for the drug preparation.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-right-time">The Right Time<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-right-time" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Under normal circumstances the right time for drug administration is not indicated by the doctor. The doctor only indicates the number of times a day a drug is to be given. For example, the doctor might state:</p>
<ul>
<li>The hourly interval between doses</li>
<li>The relation of the dose to the client's activity patterns, such as, before or after meals, on rising or retiring or every 4 hours, 6 hours or 12 hours</li>
</ul>
<p>Clients with poor time orientation, short-term memory defects or distracting activity schedules need some system for guiding them</p>
<p>in self medication. Most hospitals have set up routines for intervals and times for medication. Nonetheless you must be familiar with times for medications and the appropriate times for administering them.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="medication-procedures">Medication Procedures<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#medication-procedures" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Medication procedures vary from hospital to hospital but the underlying principles remain the same. For example, to ensure that the correct drug is given, drugs should be identified with a correctly written label at all steps in the procedure.</p>
<p>The rules to be followed are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Drug containers are labelled only in the pharmacy by the pharmacist. If a label becomes soiled or illegible, the container should be returned to the pharmacy for re-labelling.</li>
<li>If several drugs to be given to one patient are poured out, they may be placed in one cup provided the names of all the drugs are indicated in some way. If the patient does not take all of the drugs mixed in this way, all must be discarded and the required drug re-poured, because individual drugs cannot be identified by appearance alone for removal from the group.</li>
<li>Narcotics must always be identified by a written label. Narcotics must be carefully accounted for.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="medication-errors">Medication Errors<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#medication-errors" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Medication errors continue to be a serious problem in many health institutions despite the elaborate rituals used in administering drugs. Errors are determined in relation to the five rights of correct administration.</p>
<p>Common errors include a drug being administered to the wrong patient; wrong medication is given; the use of an inappropriate route; errors in timing and the incompatibility of conversion factors used in calculating doses.</p>
<p>Mistakes in drug administration may arise at any step in the process from the physician's order to the delivery of the dose to the patient.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="recording-and-tanscribing-medical-instructions">Recording and Tanscribing Medical Instructions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#recording-and-tanscribing-medical-instructions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Medical Instructions should be written by the doctor.</p>
<p>Telephone Medical Instructions and verbal Medical Instructions are only permitted under</p>
<p>Bishops Computers
Bishops Computers</p>
<p>certain circumstances. For example, if the physician is scrubbed up for a surgical procedure, the Medical Instructions may be written by a nurse, provided it is countersigned by the doctor as soon as possible.</p>
<p>The nurse who takes a spoken Medical Instructions should always read it back to the doctor for his verification. If pronunciation is unclear, the drug name should be spelled out to ensure accuracy.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="recording-and-transcribing-orders">Recording and Transcribing Orders<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#recording-and-transcribing-orders" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Drug orders should always include:</p>
<ul>
<li>The name of the patient</li>
<li>The name of the drug</li>
<li>The dose</li>
<li>The route of administration</li>
<li>The frequency or timing</li>
</ul>
<p>You should always ask the doctor to verify the route desired if no route has been specified.</p>
<p>The handwriting of many doctors may not legible. If there is doubt about any element of a drug order, oral verification must be sought from the doctor involved. Written orders may contain errors in dose and/or drug form. If any part of the order appears inappropriate, contact the doctor to correct any mistakes that may have been made.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="labelling-of-drugs">Labelling of Drugs<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#labelling-of-drugs" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Pharmacy labels on drug containers may not use the same terms used by the doctor's order. For instance, the doctor may have ordered the drug by its trade name, but the pharmacy may dispense it by its generic name.</p>
<p>Additionally, most pharmacists label preparations with metric doses where many doctors use apothecaries' doses.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="identification-of-the-patient">Identification of the Patient<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#identification-of-the-patient" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>When the patient does not have an identification band, call the patient by his name appropriately.</p>
<p>Patients who are unable to respond with their names due to disorientation, expressive aphasia or altered level of consciousness should wear a visible name label at all times.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="incomplete-delivery-of-drugs">Incomplete Delivery of Drugs<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#incomplete-delivery-of-drugs" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Drugs must reach the site of action to be effective. This may fail due to mal-absorption in the injection site, if the site is scarred, oedematous or hypoxic.</p>
<p>Another reason could be abnormally rapid metabolism and excretion. Other times problems may arise from lack of skill in delivery techniques.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="verification-errors">Verification Errors<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#verification-errors" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There are certain steps designed to detect errors so that they can be corrected before the drug is administered to a patient.</p>
<p>The following steps provide a general guideline:</p>
<ul>
<li>Compare the orders on the medication cards or cardex with the original orders written by the doctor.</li>
<li>Compare the patient's name on written records with the name tag.</li>
<li>Repeatedly read drug labels.</li>
<li>Make a conscious effort to concentrate on the task at hand if routine 'checks' are to be effective.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="reporting-errors">Reporting Errors<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#reporting-errors" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Report an error immediately it occurs. Examples of potential errors include wrong drug, an incorrect dose, the wrong patient, the wrong route used or a drug given at the wrong time. It is absolutely necessary to report it. This helps to protect both the patient and the nurse.</p>
<p><strong>Fast reporting of medication errors means that emergency measures can be taken and undesirable complications may be prevented.</strong></p>
<p>For effective drug therapy and the delivery of accurate doses of active chemicals to the body tissues at the appropriate site of action of the drug involved, you must master the following skills:</p>
<ul>
<li>Proper storage and handling of drugs</li>
<li>Command of the language used in drug therapy</li>
<li>Accurate computation of drug doses</li>
<li>Techniques used in delivering drugs by specific routes to specific sites</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="storage-and-handling-of-drugs">Storage and Handling of Drugs<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#storage-and-handling-of-drugs" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="preservation">Preservation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#preservation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Drug substances require careful storage and handling to maintain their safety and potency.</p>
<p>All drugs must be kept in a special place and secured from access by unauthorised persons. Storage areas should be kept cool and dry. This is because chemical deterioration is</p>
<p>Bishops Computers
Bishops Computers</p>
<p>enhanced by heat, moisture and light. Water dissolves solid drugs and heat melts the waxy bases of suppositories and ointment.</p>
<p>Ensure that you protect sterile substances from bacterial contamination. Inspect stocks periodically, and discard any drugs whose recommended shelf life has expired or that have changed in appearance.</p>
<p>Finally, keep storage areas clean and orderly.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="containers">Containers<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#containers" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Drugs are best kept in their original containers. Note that copying labelling may result in transcription errors. You should use original containers that protect their contents. For example, light sensitive compounds are packed in amber bottles or in containers that filter out much of the harmful radiation.</p>
<p>Do not transfer sterile substances from container to container as it increases the probability of contamination. Protect the label from soiling to ensure it remains legible.</p>
<p><strong>Remember:</strong>
Safe drug use requires that medicines have clear, accurate labels at all times.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="drug-reaction-and-interaction">Drug Reaction and Interaction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#drug-reaction-and-interaction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Any physiologically active drug has the potential to cause an undesirable reaction that may induce illness in the recipient.</p>
<p>Adverse reactions include toxic reactions, side effects, allergic reactions, cumulative reactions, tolerance and dependence and detrimental drug interactions</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="drug-interaction">Drug Interaction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#drug-interaction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A drug interaction is when an interactant chemical modifies the therapeutic results that are anticipated with a drug. The interactant may be:</p>
<ul>
<li>Another drug</li>
<li>Some combination of drugs</li>
<li>Natural or artificial chemical components of the diet</li>
<li>Pollutant chemicals from the environment</li>
<li>Endogenous body chemical</li>
<li>Chemicals used for diagnostic laboratory tests</li>
</ul>
<p>Chances of a drug interaction increase with the number of drugs the patient is taking or when the patient consults more than one physician and a variety of medications are prescribed.</p>
<p>Drug interactions are varied. They may be</p>
<p>beneficial or detrimental, and may vary from person to person. They may be of major clinical significance or of no clinical significance. They may affect absorption, distribution metabolism or excretion of drugs.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="drug-reactions">Drug Reactions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#drug-reactions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Here is a list of all the different categories of drug reactions.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="adverse-reactions">Adverse Reactions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#adverse-reactions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Adverse reactions to drugs vary in the length of time to develop. Some become apparent immediately, others appear weeks or months later. They may range from mild to catastrophic in severity. They can affect any tissue or organ.</p>
<p>Under normal circumstances, clients receiving relatively new drugs are particularly vulnerable to unexpected complications but adverse reactions to drugs that have been well tolerated for extended periods of time can occur, owing to changes in the ability of the client to metabolise or excrete the drug or because of recurrent illness. The slow accumulation of some drugs produces delayed</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="toxic-reactions">Toxic Reactions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#toxic-reactions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>You have learned a lot about this. Always remember, there is no useful drug that is completely devoid of toxic potential.</p>
<p>Think of all the toxic effects you have observed in the course of your practice.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="side-effects">Side Effects<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#side-effects" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>These have also been mentioned earlier. As we noted there are various side effects of the commonly used drugs for your patients.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="allergic-reactions">Allergic Reactions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#allergic-reactions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Allergic reactions to drugs are the result of the body's immunologic response to a drug following previous exposure to that same drug. Allergic reactions do not occur during the first exposure to a drug.</p>
<p>Allergic reactions to drugs account for up to 10% of all drug reactions. An allergic reaction may be triggered by the drug in its unchanged form, by a drug metabolic, or by inert ingredients used in drug manufacture. Injected penicillin is a primary offender in allergic reactions to drugs</p>
<p>Common allergic reactions include Urticaria</p>
<p>Bishops Computers
Bishops Computers</p>
<p>(hives) and Anaphylactic shock (involving cardiovascular and respiratory systems). This is characterised by dyspnoea and respiratory difficulty. Cough, cyanosis, angio-oedma, urticaria, pulse variations, hypotension, sometime convulsions, unconsciousness and acute cardiovascular collapse are other common reactions. The reaction is due to contraction of smooth muscles and increased vascular permeability.</p>
<p>The treatment of allergic reactions depends on their severity. Drugs used to counteract reactions include antihistamine diphenhydramine (Benadryl) for urticaria and epinephrine, antihistamine and bronchodilators for anaphylactic shock.</p>
<p>Allergic reactions to drugs are more common in people with a history of allergies, for instance, food allergies. Allergic tendencies are also familial. A family history of allergies to drugs should be noted as significant.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="idiosyncratic-reactions">Idiosyncratic Reactions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#idiosyncratic-reactions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Idiosyncratic reactions are defined as genetically determined, unexpected responses to a drug. The response may take the form of extreme sensitivity to low doses or extreme insensitivity to high doses to the drug, for example, haemolytic anaemia after treatment with primaquine.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="chain-reactions">Chain Reactions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chain-reactions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Medications are often added to a regimen to control side effects of other drugs. This can initiate a chain reaction, e.g. when cortisone is prescribed to treat a serious inflammatory condition it can cause hypertension, ulcers, diabetes and a reactivation of arrested tuberculosis. The patient may be put on diuretics, antacids or cimetidine, insulin or an antituberculous agent. Isoniacid should be prescribed with vitamin B6 to prevent a deficiency state, which can be induced by this drug.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="cumulative-reactions">Cumulative Reactions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#cumulative-reactions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Drugs accumulate in the body whenever the dosage exceeds the amount the body can eliminate through metabolism or excretion.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="tolerance-and-dependence">Tolerance and Dependence<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tolerance-and-dependence" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Habitual use of drugs may create a tolerance to the drug in use, as well as other related drugs. Habitual use of drugs can also produce physical dependence.</p>
<p>The dependent individual will develop signs and symptoms of illness when the drug is withdrawn. Withdrawal is always uncomfortable and can be dangerous. Many substances induce both tolerance and physical dependence.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="checkpoint-question">Checkpoint Question<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#checkpoint-question" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A drug interaction is when an interactant chemical modifies the therapeutic results that are anticipated with a drug.</p>
<p>Possible interactants are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Another drug</li>
<li>Some combination of drugs</li>
<li>Natural or artificial chemical components of the diet</li>
<li>Pollutant chemicals from the environment</li>
<li>Endogenous body chemical</li>
<li>Chemicals used for diagnostic laboratory tests</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="mechanisms-of-drug-interaction">Mechanisms of Drug Interaction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mechanisms-of-drug-interaction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Drugs interact in several ways.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="absorption">Absorption<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#absorption" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Absorption is the process by which a drug passes from the site of application into the central compartment of circulating fluids (the blood stream and lymphatic system). All drugs except those applied topically must be absorbed before they can produce their characteristic actions on the body. Oral administration of drugs is the most commonly employed route. 80% of all prescription and non-prescription drugs are administered orally. The mucosal membrane along the alimentary tract is a potential absorptive surface. The thin epithelial wall has high degree vascularity, which favours rapid absorption.</p>
<p>Absorption in the mouth and oesophagus is minimal. The stomach variable is dependent on acid environment, the presence or absence of foods, the amount and composition of gastric secretions, gastric motility and gastric emptying time.</p>
<p>The low pH of the stomach fluids promotes absorption of weak acids but inhibits absorption of weak bases because of the relationship between pH and ionisation. Hence, you need to know which drugs are to be taken with meals, and which on an empty stomach.</p>
<p>You must provide specific instructions to your patients as to when drugs are to be taken in relation to food ingestion. For example, insulin and other drugs that are proteins are broken down by digestive enzymes and, thus, are ineffective if given orally. Penicillin is unstable</p>
<p>Bishops Computers
Bishops Computers</p>
<p>in acid and can, therefore, be destroyed by gastric acid.</p>
<p>Gastric motility promotes disintegration of tablets to a variable extent. Any factor that slows gastric empting time will decrease and prolong the absorption of most drugs. Many drugs are given on an empty stomach with sufficient water to ensure rapid passage into the intestines. You should instruct your client accordingly.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="distribution">Distribution<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#distribution" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The process of distribution is normally underway as a drug is being absorbed into the blood supply. The presence of the drug in the blood supply makes the drug available to all parts of the body. Distribution in various fluid and tissue compartments is unlikely to be uniform because of differences in permeability of various penetration barriers, regional variation in pH and perfusion.</p>
<p>Differences in solubility of the drug by protein binding or carrier mediate transport into specific tissues. Distribution is important because it determines which tissues will be potential sites of action for the drug and the volume of fluid into which the drug will be diluted.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="inactivation">Inactivation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#inactivation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Drugs are inactivated by metabolism and excretion. The removal of the drug from plasma by either process is called clearance.</p>
<p>Renal clearance is accomplished by the kidneys. Metabolic clearance is accomplished mainly in the liver.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="metabolism">Metabolism<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#metabolism" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Metabolism is the breakdown of a substance through chemical reactions that are controlled by enzymes.</p>
<p>Metabolism of many drugs occurs largely in the liver, although some drugs are metabolised in other tissues including the nervous system, the lungs, the intestines, the placenta, the kidneys and plasma.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="excretion">Excretion<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#excretion" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The kidney is the second major avenue for drug elimination. The kidney often excretes many drugs unchanged. Kidneys remove substances from the blood by filtration and active secretion.</p>
<p>Read more about drug excretion. You will be able to identify which drugs are excreted through the kidneys, lungs, skin, milk and other secretions.</p>
<p><strong>Remember: If a patient is in shock, intramuscular injection may be ineffective because of poor perfusion of the muscle tissue.</strong></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="checkpoint-question">Checkpoint Question<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#checkpoint-question" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-is-the-definition-of-absorption">What is the definition of absorption?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-is-the-definition-of-absorption" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Absorption is the process by which a drug passes from the site of application into the central compartment of circulating fluids (the blood stream and lymphatic system).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="principles-of-the-essential-drug-kit">Principles of the Essential Drug Kit<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#principles-of-the-essential-drug-kit" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>You may have heard about the Kenya Essential Drug Kit (May 1993).</p>
<p>These Drug Kits are being packed at the Kenya Medical Supplies Agencies (KEMSA). The kits are delivered to:</p>
<ul>
<li>Provincial General Hospitals (PGH)</li>
<li>District Hospital (DH)</li>
<li>Sub District Hospitals (SDH)</li>
<li>Health Centre (HC)</li>
<li>Dispensaries (DISP)</li>
<li>Community Health workers (CHW)</li>
</ul>
<p>The kits contain drugs in use to treat common conditions in specific areas.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="essential-drugs-list">Essential Drugs List<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#essential-drugs-list" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>

<p>The units of the Pack are in: ampoules, vials, bottles, tubes, cylinders, cartons, and sets. The drugs available at the dispensary or health centre are those for treating minor ailments. Drugs requiring a doctor's prescription are only available from the Sub-District hospital.</p>
<p>The main principle of the Drug Kit is to make drugs accessible to all health facilities. You are required to adhere to the following principles as soon as you receive the drugs.</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Drugs should not stay on the shelf until they expire</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>You should always use the drugs that arrive first</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Use those drugs that are about to expire</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>All drugs that are not commonly used at the health facility should be returned so that they are redistributed</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Drugs that have expired must be returned to the pharmacy</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Check with the district public health nurse the operational principles in your district.</p>
